<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e40030b3fa1c4680bf9cd8f4db47eef2">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '49b85416-2613-4eed-8261-99706640582d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d74434230" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The high theoretical capacities and excellent redox activities motivate transitional metal sulfides (TMSs) to serve as promising anode materials for sodium-ion batteries. However, TMSs would experience low electronic conductivity as well as notorious polysulfides dissolution and shuttle effect during charge/discharge processes, which leads to unsatisfactory rate capability and cycling stability. Herein, TMSs-based anode materials with NiS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles tightly anchoring on nitrogen-doped graphene (NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG) via the Ni–N covalent bond have been developed through an electrostatic self-assembly approach between exfoliated positively charged layered double hydroxide and negatively charged graphene oxide nanosheets, followed by a sulfidation process. The strong coupling between conductive and active components enables NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG to possess good structural integrity, high ion/electron conductivity, and strong polysulfides adsorption capability, ensuring fast reaction kinetics and energetically stable performance. In consequence, the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG delivers a high capacity of 664 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, good rate performance of 545 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and excellent cycling stability with a retained capacity of 589.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1200 cycles at 0.5 A g<sup>−1</sup>, among the best results of reported TMSs-based anodes. The study provides an effective strategy to design heterostructured materials with strong coupling interaction for high-efficient-stable sodium storage.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403166-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403166-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as one of the most promising candidates for next-generation grid-oriented energy storage due to the advantages of the raw material abundance and cost, the similar electrochemical reaction mechanism to lithium-ion batteries, and the close reduction potential of sodium (−2.71&nbsp;V vs standard hydrogen electrode (SHE)) to lithium (−3.04&nbsp;V vs SHE).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0001_R_d74434222e426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Energy Small Carbon Energy Electrochem. Energy Rev. Adv. Energy Mater. Small Sci.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Over the past decades, tremendous efforts have been devoted to developing electrode materials for highly reversible and fast sodiation/desodiation process when considering the relatively larger size of Na<sup>+</sup> cation (1.02&nbsp;Å) than Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76&nbsp;Å) which always causes sluggish reaction kinetics for ion diffusion and severe structural distortion of electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0002_R_d74434222e435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Eng. J. Energy Storage Mater. Cell Rep. Phys. Sci. Small Struct. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>At present, carbonaceous materials,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0003_R_d74434222e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> alloyed metals,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0004_R_d74434222e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nano Lett. Energy Storage Mater.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and transition metal compounds<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0005_R_d74434222e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been investigated as anode materials for SIBs. Among them, transition metal sulfides (TMSs), such as NiS<sub>x</sub>, CoS<sub>x</sub>, FeS<sub>x</sub>, MoS<sub>x</sub>, etc., are the research hot spots due to the superiorities of high theoretical capacities and excellent redox activities via multiple electrons-involved conversion reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0006_R_d74434222e468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Sci. China Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Especially, NiS<sub>2</sub> has attracted intensive attention because of its high theoretical capacity (879&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) based on a four-electron conversion reaction, natural abundance, and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0007_R_d74434222e477" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Small Small Methods Mater. Today">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Unfortunately, the low conductivity and large volume change during the sodiation/desodiation processes of NiS<sub>2</sub> could cause rapid capacity fading and sluggish reaction kinetics, resulting in inferior cycling stability and rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0008_R_d74434222e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, some soluble polysulfide intermediates may be generated during the redox reaction, thus leading to the loss of active materials and degradation of capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0009_R_d74434222e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Small Methods Adv. Funct. Mater. ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Combining nanostructured TMSs with highly conductive carbon matrixes has been considered to improve the electronic conductivity and structural stability of materials to some extent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0010_R_d74434222e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Nano Adv. Mater.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the weak interaction between TMSs and carbon matrixes remains a bottleneck to be tackled, which would cause difficulty in the full utilization of the two parts, leading to unsatisfactory sodium storage performance.</p>
         
         <p>Layered double hydroxides (LDHs), a class of typical two-dimensional anionic clays consisting of positively-charged host layers and exchangeable interlayer anions with a formula of [M<sup>2+</sup><i><sub>1-x</sub></i>M<sup>3+</sup><i><sub>x</sub></i>(OH)<sub>2</sub>](A<i><sup>n−</sup></i>)<i><sub>x/n</sub></i>∙<i>y</i>H<sub>2</sub>O (M<sup>2+</sup> and M<sup>3+</sup> represent divalent and trivalent metal cations, respectively; A<i><sup>n−</sup></i> is the <i>n</i>-valent interlayer anions),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0011_R_d74434222e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have been considered as one of the promising precursors for preparing TMSs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0012_R_d74434222e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Energy Lett.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, graphene oxide (GO), another typical two-dimensional carbon material with abundant hydrophilic surface groups on the surface, is often utilized for synthesizing highly conductive graphene, or especially hetero-doped graphene nanosheets which would further enhance the electrochemical performance of TMSs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0013_R_d74434222e546" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Res.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is worth noting that LDHs and GO can be exfoliated into positively and negatively charged unilamellar sheets, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0014_R_d74434222e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Inspired by the aforementioned considerations, we developed an anode material with NiS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles robust anchoring on N-doped graphene (NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG) via the electrostatic self-assembly of exfoliated negatively charged GO and positively charged LDH nanosheets and the coupling of Ni─N covalent bond after the subsequent sulfidation process. The NG in the heterostructured NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG could effectively improve the electronic conductivity and alleviate volume change of NiS<sub>2</sub>, as well as enhance the chemisorption ability for Na<sub>2</sub>S and sodium polysulfides, as evidenced by experimental results and theoretical calculations, leading to superior electrochemical performance of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. As a result, the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG delivers high capacity (664&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), good rate performance (545&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and stable cycling behavior with a capacity retention of 85% after 1200 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, outperforming most reported TMSs SIB anode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403166-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403166-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG composite is fabricated via an electrostatic self-assembly technique with the subsequent sulfidation process, as illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0001">1a</a>. First, monometallic Ni<sup>2+</sup>Ni<sup>3+</sup>-LDH prepared using an oxidation intercalation process was exfoliated into positively charged nanosheets in formamide to form a stable light green colloidal suspension (ex-LDH). As observed from the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0001">1b</a>, the Ni<sup>2+</sup>Ni<sup>3+</sup>-LDH shows a plate-like structure with an average lateral size of ≈150&nbsp;nm. All peaks in the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a>, Supporting Information) can be indexed into hexagonal Ni(OH)<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS card No. 14–0117).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0015_R_d74434222e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Small">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1b</a> (Supporting Information) was characterized to analyze the chemical states of Ni element. The first doublet peaks at 854.1 and 855.6&nbsp;eV are ascribed to the Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub> of Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>, respectively, and the second doublet peaks at 871.9 and 873.3&nbsp;eV can be assigned to the Ni 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0008_R_d74434222e636" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ≈27.9% of Ni<sup>2+</sup> ions are estimated to be oxidized to Ni<sup>3+</sup> under the hydrothermal condition. In addition, the colloidal suspension possesses a zeta potential of ≈+50&nbsp;mV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0001">1c</a>), and a clear Tyndall light scattering effect can be observed by a red light beam (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1c</a>, Supporting Information), indicating the presence of exfoliated LDH nanosheets in formamide.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403166-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/445a245c-ff96-4811-8a12-b0a426340014/adfm202403166-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/445a245c-ff96-4811-8a12-b0a426340014/adfm202403166-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/7eb3ef86-5d2b-4f1e-aa4c-edae2c807d7e/adfm202403166-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/445a245c-ff96-4811-8a12-b0a426340014/adfm202403166-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403166-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403166" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. SEM images of b) Ni<sup>2+</sup>Ni<sup>3+</sup>-LDH, d) GO, and f) GO/LDH (inset: digital photograph of flocculent GO/LDH suspension). Zeta potential measurements and digital photographs of c) ex-LDH suspension and e) ex-GO suspension. g) XRD pattern of GO/LDH.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Meanwhile, graphene oxide was also exfoliated into negatively charged nanosheets in water to obtain a yellow-brown ex-GO dispersion, as confirmed by the SEM image and zeta potential measurements in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0001">1d,e</a>, in which a typical wrinkled structure with a size of micrometers and a zeta potential of ≈-40&nbsp;mV can be detected. After mixing the opposite-charged dispersions together, a pale-brown flocculent precipitate (GO/LDH) was settled out immediately (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0001">1f</a>) due to the intermolecular electrostatic interaction between ex-LDH and ex-GO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0016_R_d74434222e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - ACS Energy Lett.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0001">1f</a>, the LDH nanosheets lie flat against the surface of GO due to the face-to-face heterostacking manner, guaranteeing sufficient contact between them; and the XRD pattern demonstrates the existence of LDH and GO (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0001">1g</a>). Raman spectra in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information) exhibit the disordered structure (D band) at 1345&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and graphitic structure (G band) at 1585&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0017_R_d74434222e706" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Chem. Eng. J.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with a similar <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> intensity ratio of ≈0.85 for both GO and GO/LDH, indicating that the introduction of LDH do not significantly change the surface defects of GO. Finally, the heterostructured NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG can be obtained through an efficient microwave sulfidation of GO/LDH with the existence of thioacetamide (TAA), in which monometallic Ni<sup>2+</sup>Ni<sup>3+</sup>-LDH can be converted to NiS<sub>2</sub>, and graphene oxide was reduced into nitrogen-doped graphene. Significantly, the thorough contact between ex-LDH and ex-GO based on the electrostatic self-assembly can ensure the homogeneous dispersion of NiS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles on NG after sulfidation, leading to the full utilization of active material as well as effective ion/electron diffusion. Moreover, TAA can enable graphene to be doped with N species after sulfidation which could form Ni─N covalent bond with NiS<sub>2</sub>. For comparison, NiS<sub>2</sub>/un-doped graphene (NiS<sub>2</sub>/G) composite was prepared following the similar procedure with Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> as the sulfidation agent; and pure NiS<sub>2</sub> was also fabricated in the absence of GO and using thiourea as the sulfidation agent.</p>
         
         <p>The morphology and structure features of samples NiS<sub>2</sub>, NiS<sub>2</sub>/G, and NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG were investigated by SEM and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0002">2a</a>, the pure NiS<sub>2</sub> is composed of clusters assembled by nanocubes with an average size of ≈200&nbsp;nm. The aggregation of active materials is believed to restrict the ion diffusion, which would lead to depressed electrochemical performance.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403166-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/d25a6da7-0826-4009-a8fe-3b3d2fd806ff/adfm202403166-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/d25a6da7-0826-4009-a8fe-3b3d2fd806ff/adfm202403166-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/178a89f2-2cf4-4bb0-80fa-5a7327a32168/adfm202403166-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/d25a6da7-0826-4009-a8fe-3b3d2fd806ff/adfm202403166-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403166-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403166" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images of a) NiS<sub>2</sub>, b) NiS<sub>2</sub>/G, and c) NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. d) TEM and e) HRTEM of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG, accompanied with relative intensity profiles of the lattice fringes of NiS<sub>2</sub> and N-doped graphene. f) EDS elemental mappings of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0002">2b</a>, it can be observed that NiS<sub>2</sub>/G exhibits a decorated structure where incomplete cubic NiS<sub>2</sub> particles with edge lengths of hundreds of nanometers are dispersed on the surface of crumpled graphene nanosheets. While for NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG, smaller NiS<sub>2</sub> particles with sizes lower than 150&nbsp;nm are homogeneously wrapped by N-doped graphene nanosheets, as exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0002">2c,d</a>. The structure can be conducive to the reduction of side reactions between the electrolyte and electrode, and form a stable solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer, resulting in a high initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0018_R_d74434222e814" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The combination of NiS<sub>2</sub> and NG can be further demonstrated by the high-resolution TEM image in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0002">2e</a>. The clear lattice fringe of 0.283&nbsp;nm in the inner part corresponds to the (200) plane of NiS<sub>2</sub>, and the lattice spacing of 0.390 and 0.422&nbsp;nm in the outer part can be attributed to the interplanar spacing of N-doped graphene, which is found to be larger than that of standard graphene material due to N-doping effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0019_R_d74434222e827" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The elemental signals of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG can be identified from the energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping, in which a homogeneous distribution of Ni, S, C, and N elements can be clearly detected (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0002">2f</a>).</p>
         
         <p>Powder XRD measurements were conducted to examine the crystal and phase structures of NiS<sub>2</sub>, NiS<sub>2</sub>/G, and NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG, as depicted in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3a</a>. The diffraction peaks of all samples are well indexed to the cubic NiS<sub>2</sub> phase with Pa-3 (205) space group (PDF# 65–3325). Some noticeable peaks at 27.2°, 31.5°, 35.3°, 38.8°, 45.1°, and 53.5° correspond to (111), (200), (210), (211), (220), and (311) lattice planes, respectively. As expected, the broad peak with low intensity centered at ≈23° for NiS<sub>2</sub>/G and NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG can be assigned to the (002) plane of graphene nanosheets. Raman spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3b</a> were also performed to demonstrate the existence of carbon material in NiS<sub>2</sub>/G and NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG, in which the characteristic D band and G band can be observed at ≈1345 and 1595&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. And the larger <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value for sample NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG (1.1) than NiS<sub>2</sub>/G (0.94) and GO/LDH (0.85), indicating the reduction of GO to graphene, and a higher disordered degree caused by N-doping, which could affect the electron conductivity of the material. In addition, the peaks located at around 277 and 471&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> for all samples reveal the characteristic E<sub>g</sub> and A<sub>1g</sub> modes of NiS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0020_R_d74434222e888" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Mater. Adv.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403166-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b5a0fd8f-a23f-4c0a-b62f-07ab20743bef/adfm202403166-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b5a0fd8f-a23f-4c0a-b62f-07ab20743bef/adfm202403166-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/86711602-c5cc-4ac4-9f68-7f8110106714/adfm202403166-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b5a0fd8f-a23f-4c0a-b62f-07ab20743bef/adfm202403166-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403166-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403166" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns, b) Raman spectra of NiS<sub>2</sub>, NiS<sub>2</sub>/G, and NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. XPS c) survey, d) Ni 2p, and e) S 2p spectra of NiS<sub>2</sub>/G and NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. XPS f) N 1s and g) C 1s spectrum of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. h) TG analysis, and i) nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>XPS was measured to analyze the differences in chemical composition and surface electronic state of NiS<sub>2</sub>/G and NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. As observed from the survey spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3c</a>, both materials are mainly composed of Ni, S, C, and O elements. In particular, the signal of N element can be detected for NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG due to the existence of N-doped graphene.</p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3d</a> compares the high-resolution spectra of Ni 2p. Specifically, peaks of NiS<sub>2</sub>/G at 853.82 and 854.91&nbsp;eV are assigned to the Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub> of Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>, respectively, and peaks at 871.22 and 872.31&nbsp;eV are indexed to the Ni 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of Ni<sup>2+</sup> and Ni<sup>3+</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0021_R_d74434222e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Besides, the other two peaks located at 859.52 and 877.72&nbsp;eV are ascribed to the shakeup satellites. In comparison, the Ni 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Ni 2p<sub>1/2</sub> peaks for NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG shift to higher binding energies with ≈0.2&nbsp;eV. Significantly, two additional peaks at 853.23 and 872&nbsp;eV can also be found, which arise from the Ni─N covalent bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0022_R_d74434222e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Eng. J.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the S 2p spectrum of NiS<sub>2</sub>/G in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3e</a>, the two major peaks at 162.91 and 164.11&nbsp;eV correspond to S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub>, respectively, while the other two peaks (165.25 and 168.05&nbsp;eV) belong to the oxidized sulfur S─O bonds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0023_R_d74434222e987" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Storage Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As expected, ≈0.2&nbsp;eV shifts toward lower binding energies can be seen for NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. The positive shift of Ni 2p and the negative shift of S 2p reveal the strong interactions between NiS<sub>2</sub> and N-doped graphene in NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0024_R_d74434222e999" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3f</a>, the high-resolution XPS spectrum of N 1s contains three characteristic types of N: pyridinic N at 397.4&nbsp;eV, pyrrolic N at 399.75&nbsp;eV, and graphitic N at 401.1&nbsp;eV, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0025_R_d74434222e1007" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Natl. Sci. Rev.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Notably, there exists another peak located at 398.17&nbsp;eV, which can be assigned to the N-Ni bond.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0022_R_d74434222e1013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Chem. Eng. J.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the high-resolution C 1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3g</a>), the C─N bond can be detected at a peak of 285.7&nbsp;eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0026_R_d74434222e1021" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Nano-Micro Lett.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The results manifest that the successful doping of N species in graphene can induce the covalent coupling interaction with NiS<sub>2</sub> and effectively tune the surface electronic states, ensuring the structural stability of the material. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was employed in an air atmosphere to evaluate the NiS<sub>2</sub> and carbon contents in NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. According to the result in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3h</a> with 42.5 wt.% of origin mass retained at 900&nbsp;°C, the mass fractions of NiS<sub>2</sub> and N-doped graphene can be calculated to be 69.9 and 30.1&nbsp;wt.%, respectively. The pore structure and surface area of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG were investigated by nitrogen adsorption/desorption measurement, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3i</a>. The curve identifies a typical type IV isotherm with an H3 type hysteresis loop in the range of 0.5–1.0 P/P<sub>0</sub>, revealing the existence of mesopores,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0027_R_d74434222e1046" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Mater. Interfaces">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which can also be demonstrated from the pore size distribution curve in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0003">3i</a>. The average pore diameter, Brunauer-Emmett-Teller (BET) surface area, and total pore volume of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG are ≈10.7&nbsp;nm, 28.1&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and 0.162&nbsp;cm<sup>3</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The mesoporous feature as well as the modest surface area should be conducive to the full infiltration of electrolytes and effective Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion during the electrochemical storage process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0006_R_d74434222e1067" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater. Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Sci. China Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The sodium storage performance of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG was first measured in sodium ion coin-type half-cells in a voltage range of 0.01–2.6&nbsp;V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the initial three cyclic-voltammetry (CV) curves of the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. At the first cycle, the cathodic peaks at around 1.26, 0.91, 0.72, and 0.38&nbsp;V correspond to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into NiS<sub>2</sub> to form Na<sub>x</sub>NiS<sub>2</sub>, the transformation of Na<sub>x</sub>NiS<sub>2</sub> to Ni and Na<sub>2</sub>S, as well as the formation of SEI films from the electrolyte decomposition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0018_R_d74434222e1104" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the subsequent first anodic scan, peaks located at around 1.75 and 1.97&nbsp;V represent the reversible formation of Na<sub>x</sub>NiS<sub>2</sub> from Ni and Na<sub>2</sub>S, and the peak at around 2.15&nbsp;V indicates the regeneration of NiS<sub>2</sub> within the desodiation process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0028_R_d74434222e1118" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Mater. Chem. A">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The reactions are as follows:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0018_R_d74434222e1123" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403166-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/82a19fe1-f345-4738-8e7b-b581bd30889d/adfm202403166-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/82a19fe1-f345-4738-8e7b-b581bd30889d/adfm202403166-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6c31acc0-4884-4677-a721-9a6139e36e7d/adfm202403166-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/82a19fe1-f345-4738-8e7b-b581bd30889d/adfm202403166-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403166-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403166" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The initial three CV curves of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. (b) The initial three GCD profiles of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode at a current density of 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Cycling stability of NiS<sub>2</sub>, NiS<sub>2</sub>/G, and NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 100 cycles. d) GCD curves and e) rate capability of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG at various current densities. f) Comparison of the rate capability of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG with other reported Ni<sub>x</sub>S<sub>y</sub>-based electrodes for SIBs. g) Long-term cycling performance of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG, NiS<sub>2</sub>/G, and NiS<sub>2</sub> at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. h) Digital photographs of the postcycled electrodes/separators, and top-view SEM images of postcycled electrode surface.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>It can be observed that the CV curves nearly overlap for the subsequent second and third cycles with the cathodic peaks located at around 1.54, 0.97, and 0.86&nbsp;V, and the anodic peaks at around 1.69, 1.89, and 1.99&nbsp;V, respectively, demonstrating the good reversibility of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0029_R_d74434222e1288" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Eng. J.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Correspondingly, the initial three galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) curves of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0004">4b</a> also present similar charge/discharge platforms to the peak locations in the CV curves. The electrode exhibits a specific capacity of 836.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the first discharge process and 722.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at the subsequent charge process with an ICE of 86.4%. In contrast, the NiS<sub>2</sub>/G and NiS<sub>2</sub> electrodes deliver discharge/charge capacities of 851.0/716.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and 711.9/485.1&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with inferior ICE of 84.1% and 68.1%, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). As further cycling proceeds to 100 cycles, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0004">4c</a>, the capacity of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode can maintain at 676.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is much higher than those of NiS<sub>2</sub>/G (583.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), NiS<sub>2</sub> (350.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), and some reported Ni<sub>x</sub>S<sub>y</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0018_R_d74434222e1338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0030_R_d74434222e1338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0004">4d</a> compares the rate capabilities of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG, NiS<sub>2</sub>/G, and NiS<sub>2</sub> at different current densities from 0.1 to 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode delivers reversible capacities of 659.4, 615.1, 594.6, 569.7, 555.8, and 545.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 1.5, and 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The capacity is higher than the calculated theoretical capacity of the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG (≈614&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), which might be ascribed to the contribution of NG in the electrode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0031_R_d74434222e1371" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In contrast, the NiS<sub>2</sub> electrode displays a specific capacity of 537.7&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and 389.0&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. For the NiS<sub>2</sub>/G electrode, specific capacities of 635.1 and 509.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> can be obtained at 0.1 and 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Upon lowering the current density from 2 to 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 93.5%, 85.1%, and 89.2% of the initial capacity can be retained for NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG, NiS<sub>2</sub>/G, and NiS<sub>2</sub>, respectively, demonstrating the superior rate performance of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. The results illustrate that the heterostructure with NiS<sub>2</sub> particles robust stabilized on N-doped graphene nanosheets via electrostatic self-assembly and covalent bond strategy is quite beneficial to charge and ion transport. The corresponding GCD profiles of the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG at different current densities in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0004">4e</a> show similar shapes with obvious charge/discharge plateaus, indicating low polarizations during the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation processes with the increase of current density.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0032_R_d74434222e1414" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG also delivers remarkable superiority in rate performance when compared with most reported Ni<sub>x</sub>S<sub>y</sub>-based electrodes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0018_R_d74434222e1426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0021_R_d74434222e1426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0030_R_d74434222e1426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0033_R_d74434222e1426" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Nano Adv. Energy Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0004">4f</a>. Furthermore, even cycling at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode demonstrates excellent stability with a specific capacity of 589.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (85% retention rate) retained after 1200 continuous charge/discharge processes, outperforming the NiS<sub>2</sub>/G and NiS<sub>2</sub> electrodes (Figures&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0004">4g</a>), and previously reported nickel sulfides electrodes (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0030_R_d74434222e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Energy Mater. Chem. Eng. J. Adv. Funct. Mater.">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0034_R_d74434222e1451" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Storage Mater. Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Mater. Today Chem. Nano Energy Sci. China Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information), clear platforms/slopes can be detected from the GCD curves at various charge/discharge stages from the beginning to the end, and the curves from 50 to 1200 cycles overlap well. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), the cross-sectional SEM images of three electrodes before and after 50 cycling tests show that the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG anode exhibits the lowest thickness expansion, indicating the improved volume buffering effect.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0005_R_d74434222e1465" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, when the electrodes after cycling tests are disassembled and then dipped into fresh electrolytes, the electrolyte containing NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG anode is colorless (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information), manifesting the suppression of the shuttling effect of polysulfides during the charge/discharge processes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0035_R_d74434222e1476" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Energy Storage Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The postcycled electrodes were further used to examine the changes in electrode morphology and microstructure. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0004">4h</a> reveals that the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode can maintain its integrality after cycling with the electrode surface and the color of the glass fiber separator almost remains unchanged, confirming the prominent structural stability that arises from the unique coupling interaction with NiS<sub>2</sub> and N-doped graphene. And no macro/micro-cracks can be observed from the SEM image. For NiS<sub>2</sub>/G and NiS<sub>2</sub> electrodes, the active materials are partially peeled off from the current collector after cycling, and the separators become yellow or dark brown which could be caused by the stacking of sulfur components originating from soluble polysulfide intermediates during the conversion reaction of NiS<sub>2</sub>. Besides, massive cracking appears on the surface of both electrodes owing to particle pulverization and agglomeration.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The kinetics of sodium storage in NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG was first explored through the CV tests at low scan rates from 0.4 to 1.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, as exhibited in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0005">5a</a>. Curves with similar shapes and weak electrode polarization can be observed. The peak current (<i>i</i>) and scan rate (<i>υ</i>) follow the relationships (Equations&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403166-disp-0003" title="Link to equation">3</a> and 4):<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0036_R_d74434222e1516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Small Methods">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403166-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403166-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/def5b123-39d8-4ad3-bf6b-829215b32739/adfm202403166-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/def5b123-39d8-4ad3-bf6b-829215b32739/adfm202403166-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/69bae515-fedb-4226-aeec-4ff448ca1d8b/adfm202403166-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/def5b123-39d8-4ad3-bf6b-829215b32739/adfm202403166-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403166-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403166" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode at various scan rates ranging from 0.4 to 1.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> and b) the corresponding log(<i>i</i>)-log(<i>υ</i>) plots. c) Nyquist plots and d) fitted straight lines of Z’ versus <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> of NiS<sub>2</sub>, NiS<sub>2</sub>/G, and NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrodes. e) Calculated binding energy of NiS<sub>2</sub> on G and NG. f) Simulation of G and NG with Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> polysulfides, and g) calculated adsorption energy of Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> polysulfides on G and NG surface.</div>

            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>After fitting the log(<i>i</i>)−log(<i>v</i>) plots in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0005">5b</a>, the corresponding <i>b</i> values from peak 1 to peak 4 obtained from the slopes are 0.74, 0.87, 0.69, and 0.97, respectively, implying the combination of capacitive and diffusion-controlled behaviors in NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode during the sodiation/desodiation processes.</p>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">In addition, the current at a given potential can be expressed into capacitive current (<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>υ</i>) and the diffusion-controlled current (<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>υ</i><sup>1/2</sup>) (Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403166-disp-0005" title="Link to equation">5</a>):<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0038_R_d74434222e1651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Adv. Mater.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403166-disp-0005"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/59485247-3815-4de3-92e2-ef297fa2ec78/adfm202403166-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403166:adfm202403166-math-0005" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202403166-math-0005.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <mi>i</mi>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>k</mi>
                              <mn>1</mn>
                           </msub>

                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}i\ = {{k}_1}\ \upsilon + {{k}_2}{{\upsilon }^{1/2}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(5)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">Accordingly, the capacitive contributions are fitted to account for 90.5%, 92.7%, 93.8%, 94.7%, and 95.4% at the scan rates of 0.4, 0.6, 0.8, 1.0, and 1.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information), which represents a dominant surface capacitive process over the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode resulting from the reasonable combination of NiS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles with N-doped graphene nanosheets.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0039_R_d74434222e1696" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was carried out on electrodes to get further insights into the electrochemical kinetics. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0005">5c</a>, all curves comprise a semicircle in the high-medium frequency corresponding to the resistance of contact parameters (electrode, electrolyte. separator, SEI film) and charge transfer, and a straight line in the low frequency associated with the Warburg impedance which could reflect the sodium ion diffusion in the material. For NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG, the smallest semicircle diameter can be observed, revealing the lowest charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of 4.29&nbsp;Ω, which confirms that the combination of N-doped graphene nanosheets is beneficial to enhance the charge transfer kinetics. Moreover, according to the following Equations&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403166-disp-0006" title="Link to equation">6</a> and&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403166-disp-0007" title="Link to equation">7</a>:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0040_R_d74434222e1717" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403166-disp-0006"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/0cbd8e95-1559-4743-b230-414e18ffeca6/adfm202403166-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403166:adfm202403166-math-0006" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202403166-math-0006.png">

                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(7)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <div class="paragraph-element">The linear relationship between <i>Z</i>’ (the real part of the Nyquist plot) and <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> (<i>ω</i> is the angular frequency) can be depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0005">5d</a>, in which the Warburg coefficient (<i>σ</i>) can be obtained from the slope of the plot. Furthermore, the sodium ion diffusion coefficient (<i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>) can be calculated based on the Equation&nbsp;<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#adfm202403166-disp-0008" title="Link to equation">8</a>:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0041_R_d74434222e1785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403166-disp-0008"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c91bbe6f-65f6-42f1-b9c8-a7b5c9354dfb/adfm202403166-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403166:adfm202403166-math-0008" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202403166-math-0008.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mrow>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi>Na</mi>
                                 <mo>+</mo>
                              </mrow>
                           </msub>
                           <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">R</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                           <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">T</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                           <mo>/</mo>
                           <mn>2</mn>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi>
                              <mn>4</mn>
                           </msup>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">F</mi>
                              <mn>4</mn>
                           </msup>
                           <msup>
                              <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                           <msup>
                              <mi>σ</mi>
                              <mn>2</mn>
                           </msup>
                        </mrow>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{D}_{{\mathrm{Na}} + }} = {{{\mathrm{R}}}^2}\ {{{\mathrm{T}}}^2}/2{{{\mathrm{A}}}^2}{{{\mathrm{n}}}^4}{{{\mathrm{F}}}^4}{{{\mathrm{C}}}^2}{{\sigma }^2}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(8)</span></div>where R is the gas constant, T is the Kelvin temperature, A is the area of the electrode, n is the electronic transfer number, F is the Faraday constant, and C is the concentration of sodium ions. It is found that the smaller <i>σ</i> is, the higher <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub> can be. Specifically, the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode delivers the lowest <i>σ</i> value of 3.88, demonstrating the highest <i>D</i><sub>Na+</sub>, which is almost 2.6 and 312.7 times higher than those of NiS<sub>2</sub>/G (<i>σ</i> is 6.24) and NiS<sub>2</sub> (<i>σ</i> is 68.61), respectively.</div>
         
         <p>Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations were further carried out to elucidate the fundamental mechanisms that improve cycling performance. As exhibited in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0005">5e</a>, the binding energy between NiS<sub>2</sub> and NG is calculated to be −4.287&nbsp;eV, which is much higher than that between NiS<sub>2</sub> and G (−0.796&nbsp;eV), indicating the firm interaction caused by the Ni─N covalent bond. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0005">5f</a> displays the crystal structures of carbon monolayer containing N─C bonds (NG) or single carbon sheet (G) with sodium polysulfides Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> (x = 1, 2, 4, 6, 8). On this basis, the adsorption energies of various Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> can be calculated, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403166-fig-0005">5g</a>. The adsorption energies of Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>6</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>8</sub> on pure carbon sheet are −0.73, −0.92, −0.60, −0.57, and −0.59&nbsp;eV, respectively; while N-doped carbon exhibits adsorption energies of −1.81, −2.05, −1.45, −1.07, and −0.95&nbsp;eV for Na<sub>2</sub>S, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>4</sub>, Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>6</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, respectively. The results demonstrate that NG has a stronger adsorption capability on Na<sub>2</sub>S<sub>x</sub> species, which would effectively suppress the shuttling effect of polysulfides during the charge/discharge processes (as illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information), leading to the excellent lifetime of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG. In addition, a more negative adsorption energy (ΔE<sub>ad</sub>) value of Na atom for NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG (−4.37&nbsp;eV) than NiS<sub>2</sub>/G (−3.35&nbsp;eV) from the optimized Na-adsorption models (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that NG enables NiS<sub>2</sub> with more beneficial to the adsorption of Na. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), the charge density difference of NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG exhibits a larger accumulation region around the NG layer than that of NiS<sub>2</sub>/G around the G layer, confirming the stronger binding between NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG and Na<sup>+</sup>.</p>
         
         <p>To further demonstrate the practical application of the as-prepared NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG anode material, sodium ion full cells were assembled by coupling Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode. The negative/positive capacity ratio is set to be ≈1.2.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0042_R_d74434222e1969" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Energy">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), the Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> cathode exhibits the average charge/discharge plateau of ≈3.4&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), and maintains a stable capacity of ≈91&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. For the full cell, it can deliver a discharge capacity of 422&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>–1</sup> (based on anode material) and 200&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 and 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13a</a>, Supporting Information), which are comparable to other reported sodium ion full-cell systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403166-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403166-bib-0034_R_d74434222e2002" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Energy Storage Mater. Chem. Eng. J. J. Mater. Chem. A Mater. Today Chem. Nano Energy Sci. China Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After cycling at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for 100 cycles, a capacity of 229&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> can be retained (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13b</a>, Supporting Information)</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403166-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403166-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In this work, the electrostatic self-assembly and covalent bond strategy has been successfully applied to prepare NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG composite, which ensures the homogeneous and stable anchoring of NiS<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles on NG, leading to the full utilization of active material, effective ion/electron diffusion, strong polysulfide affinity, and effective volume change suppression, as investigated by physical characterizations, electrochemical measurements, and DFT calculations. Consequently, the NiS<sub>2</sub>/NG electrode possesses improved electrochemical performance including high specific capacity, good rate performance, and excellent cycling stability. The strategy is expected to extend to other heterostructured materials with strong coupling interaction for achieving superior electrochemical properties.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
